المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل المعاوقة فيكون بعض من الزمان بإزاء المعاوق، وبعض منه بإزاء الحركة لأجل الأمور المذكورة، وهو زمان الخلاء، فما يكون بإزاء المعاوق يتفاوت على حسب يلزم محذور كما تحققته. وقد عن الوجه الأول أيضا بأنه مبني على إمكان قوام يكون نسبة معاوقته إلى معاوقة الملء المفروض أولاً ؛ كنسبة زمان الخلاء إلى زمان الملء وهو ممنوع لجواز أن ينتهي قوام الملء إلى قوام واعتبار نسبة المعاوقتين كنسبة الزمانين بأن يقال : لو أمكن الخلاء لأمكن وقوع الحركة فيه في زمان ولو أمكن ذلك أمكن وجود حركة لا معاوقة لها مماثلة لحركة لها معاوقة ما وهو محال وهو إنما نشأ من وجود الخلاء إذ لا شبهة في إمكان ما سوى الخلاء بل في وقوعها فيكون محالاً. وهو خلاصة ما في الشفاء وبه يندفع ما قيل إنه لا نسلم وجود نسبة بين المعاوقتين كنسبة بين الزمانين لأن الأولى من العددية والثانية من المقدارية. وقد بين إقليدس أنه إذا وجد نسبة بين المقدارين لا يلزم أن يوجد تلك النسبة توجد تلك النسبة بين العددين، وكذا يندفع ما ذكره بقوله : وقد أجيب كما لا يخفى . نعم يرد عليه أنه إن فرض اتحاد المتحرك والقوة المتحركة والمسافة يختار أنه لا يمكن معاوقة مساوية أو أقل في زمان اللامعاوقة ولا يلزم من ذلك انتهاء مراتب المعاوقة في نفسها وهو ظاهر وإن لم يفرض يختار أنه يمكن وجودها ولا نسلم بطلان اللازم، وهو مساواة حركة لا معاوقة لها لحركة لها معاوقة لجواز اختلافهما في القوة المحركة . فيكون المعاوقة الخارجية معادلة بضعف القوة المحركة فيما لا معاوقة لها . قوله : ( لجواز أن ينتهي قوام الملأ إلى قوام الخ العددية والثانية من النسب المقدارية. وقد برهن إقليدس على أنه يجوز أن يكون لمقدار إلى آخر نسبة لا توجد تلك النسبة بين النسب العددية، ولك أن تنقل المنع إلى نسبة المعاوقة . وتقول : لم لا أن
The second observatory - the ninth topic: Place, which is of continuous quantity. The resistance, so some of the time is for the resistance, and some of it is for the motion due to the mentioned matters, and that is the time of void. So what is for the resistance varies according to its variation, and what is for those matters varies according to their variation, not according to the variation of resistance. And since those matters are assumed equal in the hypothesized motions under discussion, their time does not vary among them, but only what is for the resistance varies, so no absurdity follows as you have realized. And the first objection was also answered by saying that it is based on the possibility of a density whose resistance's ratio to the resistance of the assumed plenum first is like the ratio of void time to plenum time, and that is denied because it is possible that the density of the plenum ends at a density than which a thinner cannot be, and it is not such that that non-resistance or less is possible in it. On the first, it necessitates the possibility of the existence of a motion with resistance similar to a motion without resistance, and on the second, it necessitates the finitude of the degrees of resistance to a degree than which a lesser is not possible, while intuition witnesses otherwise. The Shaykh [Avicenna] said in the Shifa regarding this investigation: 'And you will later know that there is no effect except that in the nature of the moving thing it accepts less than it, if there were an effect that affects, so it must be that some of those resistances that the body's nature can bear is equal in its time to the non-resistant, and that is impossible. So it appears that there is no natural motion from the void.' And by this it appears that the proof can be formulated in a way that does not need considering three motions and considering the ratio of the two resistances as the ratio of the two times, by saying: If the void were possible, then motion in it would be possible in time, and if that were possible, then it would be possible to have a motion without resistance similar to a motion with some resistance, which is impossible. And this arises only from the existence of the void, since there is no doubt about the possibility of other than the void—rather its occurrence—so it would be impossible. That is the essence of what is in the Shifa, and by it is refuted what was said: 'We do not accept the existence of a ratio between the two resistances like the ratio between the two times, because the first is from numerical [ratios] and the second from quantitative [ratios].' Euclid has shown that if there is a ratio between two quantities, it does not necessarily follow that that ratio exists between the two numbers. Likewise, what he mentioned by his statement 'and it was answered as is not hidden' is refuted. Yes, it is objected to him: If the mover, the motive force, and the distance are assumed unified, then one chooses that there cannot be a resistance equal or less in the time of non-resistance, and that does not necessitate the finitude of the degrees of resistance in themselves, and that is apparent. And if they are not assumed unified, then one chooses that it can exist, and we do not accept the falsity of the consequent, which is the equality of a motion without resistance to a motion with resistance, because it is possible that they differ in motive force, so the external resistance is compensated by the weakness of the motive force in the one without resistance. His statement: 'Because it is possible that the density of the plenum ends at a density...' its summary is the denial of the existence of plenums whose ratio of the thinner to the thicker is like the ratio of the time of motion in void to the time of motion of the thicker plenum, because of the possibility of the mentioned end. And if the impossibility of that end is granted, the similarity of the two ratios also does not necessarily follow, because the first is from the numerical ratios and the second from quantitative ratios. Euclid has proved that it is possible for a quantity to have a ratio to another that is not found among the numerical ratios. And you may transfer the denial to the ratio of resistance and say: Why not that...
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed argument regarding motion in a void. It begins by explaining that time of motion consists of two parts: one for resistance and one for the motion itself (due to internal factors). In void motion, since there is no resistance, the time is only due to internal factors. If these internal factors are equal across motions, then the times of void motions are equal, and no contradiction arises. The objection that this requires a proportional relationship between resistances and times is refuted: it is not necessary that such a proportion exists because densities may be at a minimum. The commentator then quotes Avicenna's argument from the Shifa: if void were possible, then a motion without resistance equal in time to a motion with resistance would be possible, which is impossible. Therefore, void motion is impossible. The commentator then discusses the response that the ratio of resistances is numerical while the ratio of times is quantitative, and Euclid's geometry shows that a quantitative ratio does not imply a numerical ratio. Further objections and responses are given, including the possibility that differences in motive force can compensate for resistance. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing the possibility of denying the ratio of resistance.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of void motion is proven solely by the proportionality of resistance and time.
Correction: The passage shows that the proportionality argument is not sufficient; Avicenna provides a stronger, more direct argument from the equality of times.

D) That void motion is faster than any plenum motion

Key Takeaway
The debate over motion in void illustrates how medieval philosophers used logical and mathematical reasoning to argue about physical possibilities, with Avicenna's refutation standing as a key objection.

?

This lesson uses rigorous logic to prove an impossibility. Do you apply the same level of reasoning to your own beliefs about faith? Where might you be accepting a 'void' – an assumption that seems easy but leads to contradiction?
?
Avicenna's proof relies on the absurdity of equating two different types of time. Can you identify areas in your life where you have mistakenly treated comparable things as equal (e.g., excuses and actions)? What would it take to correct that?

Second Proof Against Void and Its Refutation
Proof against void via preponderator, and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against the void based on the principle of preference without a preponderator, and how al-Jurjani refutes it by appealing to the world's fullness and natural affinities.

Core Idea
The void argument fails because the world fills all space and bodies settle by affinity.

Al-Jurjani presents a second proof against the void, arguing that a body in void would require arbitrary specification. After presenting the proof, he offers a refutation that appeals to the world's plenitude and the natural properties of bodies. This passage demonstrates how kalam thinkers defend the impossibility of void through logical and physical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Understand the claim that a body in void would need a preponderator to specify its location, since all parts of void are similar.
The proof considers both rest and motion.
2
Consider the two cases
A body at rest would be arbitrarily specified to one spot; a body in motion would leave one spot for another without reason. Both are instances of preference without preponderator.
3
Study the refutation
Al-Jurjani answers that the entire world fills all of the void, so there is no specification to a particular place—the world occupies the whole extent of void.
This avoids the need for a preponderator.
4
Examine the objection and response
An objector says the argument applies to parts of the world, not the whole. The response is that parts are specified by mutual affinity and repulsion of bodies, which are natural causes.
Thus specification is not arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني ) من وجوه امتناع الخلاء ( الجسم لو حصل في الخلاء) سواء كان بعداً موهوماً أو موجوداً ( كان اختصاصه بحيز دون آخر ترجيحاً بلا مرجح لتشابه أجزائه ) فإن البعد المفروض لا يتصور فيه اختلاف وكذا الحال في البعد الموجود المجرد إذ اختلاف الأمثال إنما يكون ( بالمادة فإذا فرض حصول جسم في حيز فإن كان ساكناً فيه لزم اختصاصه به من غير مرجح. وإن كان متحركاً عنه لزم تركه لحيز وطلبه لآخر مع تساويهما . وذلك أيضاً نوع اختصاص له بالحيز الآخر، وترجيح بلا مرجح ) والجواب أن كل العالم لا اختصاص له بحيز) دون حيز ( فإنه مالئ للأحياز) كلها إذ الخلاء الذي هو المكان إنما هو بمقدار العالم فيمتلئ به فلا اختصاص له بحيز دون آخر فلا ترجيح فإن قيل ليس كلامنا في مجموع العالم وحيزه حتى يجاب بما ذكرتموه . بل ( الكلام في كل جزء من أجزاء العالم وما يحصل فيه ذلك الجزء من الأمكنة الخلائية (قلنا : لعل الاختصاص الحاصل لأجزاء العالم بأحيازها المعينة إنما يكون ) لتلاؤم الأجسام وتنافرها )
The second of the ways of impossibility of void: (If a body occurred in void) whether it be an imaginary or existent dimension, (its specification to a particular place without another would be a preference without a preponderator due to the similarity of its parts). For the supposed dimension cannot be conceived to have difference, and similarly for the existent abstract dimension, since difference among similars only occurs (through matter. So if a body is assumed to occur in a place, if it is stationary therein, its specification to it would be without a preponderator; and if it is moving away from it, it would necessitate leaving one place and seeking another while they are equivalent. That too is a type of specification to the other place, and a preference without a preponderator.) The answer is that the entire world has no specification to one place rather than another, (for it fills all places) since the void which is place is only to the extent of the world, so it fills it, and thus it has no specification to one place rather than another, so no preference. If it is said: Our speech is not about the totality of the world and its place so that it may be answered as you have mentioned. Rather, (the speech is about every part of the world and what that part obtains of the void places). We say: Perhaps the specification that occurs for the parts of the world to their particular places is only (due to the mutual affinity of bodies and their repulsion).
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against the existence of void and its refutation. The proof is the second of several arguments. It argues that if a body were placed in void, it would require a preference without a preponderator to determine its location, because all parts of the void are similar. The argument considers both the case of a body at rest and in motion; in either case, the body would have to be specified to a particular place without any differentiating factor. The response is that the entire world fills all of the void, so it has no specification to a particular place. An objection follows: the discussion is about parts of the world, not the whole. The answer is that the specification of each part is due to the mutual affinity and repulsion of bodies, which determines their natural places. Thus the proof fails because the specification is not arbitrary but based on physical properties.

Objection from Stone Thrown Upward and Answer
Presents argument that void would allow stone to reach sky, refuted by air resistance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from a stone thrown upward is used to deny the void, and why the answer only refutes the claim that the entire space is void, not void in general.

Core Idea
The stone's failure to reach the sky disproves only full void, not partial void.

This lesson presents one of several arguments against the existence of void (al-khala'). The argument observes that a stone thrown upward stops before reaching the sky, which it would not do if space were empty of any resisting medium. The answer shows that the argument only proves that the space between earth and sky is not completely void, not that no void exists anywhere. Let us examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the argument that in a void the stone would keep moving upward continuously.
Notice the assumption that forced motion ceases only due to collisions with a resisting medium.
2
Read the answer
See how the answer distinguishes between total void and partial void, allowing for the presence of air as a resisting medium.
The answer does not deny void entirely.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this nuance matters for the broader debate on the existence of void.
The argument only works if the entire distance were empty, which is not observed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : ( من تلك الوجوه ( أنه إذا رمي حجر إلى فوق فلولا معاوقة الملء) لذلك الحجر عن الحركة الوصل إلى السماء، وذلك لأن صعوده إليها إنما هو بقوة فيه استفادها من القاسر فتلك القوة ما دامت باقية يكون الحجر متحركاً نحو الفوق وهي أعني تلك القوة لا تعدم بذاتها بل بمصادمات الملء الذي في المسافة، فإذا كانت المسافة خالية لم تعدم القوة حتى يصل إلى السماء وهو باطل بالمشاهدة ) والجواب : أنه ( أي ما ذكرتم من الدليل على تقدير صحته ( إنما ينفي كون ما بين السماء والأرض كله خلاء) . إذ حينئذ لم يكن هناك معاوقة مانعة من الوصول إلى السماء ) ولا ينفي وجود الخلاء مطلقاً ( لجواز أن يكون الغالب في هذه المسافة (الهواء الذي هو ملء معاوق يوجب ضعف الميل القسري حتى يبطل . (و) يكون مع ذلك ( فيما بينهما خلاء كثير)
The third (of those proofs) is that when a stone is thrown upward, if not for the resistance of the fill (al-mal') to that stone against motion, it would reach the sky; because its ascent to it is only by a force it acquired from the compeller, and that force as long as it remains, the stone moves upward, and that force does not cease by itself but by the collisions with the fill in the distance; so if the distance were empty, the force would not cease until it reaches the sky, and this is false by observation. And the answer: that what you mentioned of the proof, on the assumption of its validity, only negates that what is between the sky and the earth is entirely void; because then there would be no resistance preventing reaching the sky; but it does not negate the existence of void absolutely, because it is possible that the dominant in this distance is air, which is a resisting fill that causes the forced inclination to weaken until it perishes; and with that, there is much void between them.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against void (khala') from the case of a stone thrown upward. The argument: if the space were empty, the stone would reach the sky because the forced motion would not cease without resistance. Observation shows it does not, so space is not empty. The answer: the argument only shows that the space between earth and sky is not entirely void, because air (a fill) provides resistance, weakening the forced motion. It does not prove that no void exists anywhere; there could be void elsewhere and also some void in between.

Misconception: The answer denies the existence of void entirely.
Correction: The answer only denies that the entire space between earth and sky is void; it does not deny void absolutely. Air provides resistance, and void may exist elsewhere.

Key Takeaway

?

?

?
The objector thought that without resistance he would reach the sky. Do you assume that without trials you would be spiritually perfect? The Concept of the Void (Khala') in Kalam
Al-Iji defines the void and explains two positions on its emptiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the void as an existing dimension abstract from matter and the two main views among its proponents regarding whether it can be empty of a body.

Core Idea
The void is a real dimension, not nothingness, but its nature divides philosophers.

Before refuting claims about forces in the void, Al-Iji first establishes what the void is. He defines it as an existing dimension abstract from matter and notes disagreements about whether it can be occupied. Understanding these foundational positions is essential for the later refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read Al-Iji's definition of the void as an existing dimension abstract from matter, and note that it can be occupied or empty.
2
Understand the two variants
Identify the two groups among proponents: those who allow the void to be empty of a body and those who do not. Understand how this relates to the surface theory.
The surface theory posits two dimensions in a bowl.
3
Connect to later refutation
Reflect on how these definitions set the stage for rejecting forces in the void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فخلاء) أي بعد موجود مجرد في نفسه عن المادة سواء كان مشغولاً ببعد جسمي يملؤه أو غير مشغول به فإنه في نفسه خلاء ) ومنهم من جعله عدماً صرفاً كما مر) من أنه حقيقة الخلاء عند القائلين: بأنَّ المكان بعد موهوم أن يكون الجسمان بحيث لا يتلاقيان ولا يكون بينهما ما يلاقيهما ( الثاني (منهم) أي من القائلين بالخلاء أعني بالبعد الموجود المجرد في نفسه عن المادة (من جوز أن لا يملأه جسم فيكون حينئذ خلاء بمعنى أنَّه بعد مجرد عن المادة وبمعنى أنه مكان خال عن الشاغل ( ومنهم من لم يجوزه) فيكون حينئذ خلاء بالمعنى الأول دون الثاني مذهب من قال بالسطح أن فيما بين أطراف الطاس على هذا المذهب بعداً موجوداً مجرداً في نفسه عن المادة قد انطبق عليه بعد الجسم فهناك بعدان، إلا أنَّ الأول لا يجوز خلوه عن انطباق الثاني عليه . وأما على القول : بأنَّ هو السطح فليس هناك إلا بعد الجسم الذي هو في داخل الطاس . ( الثالث قال ابن زكريا في الخلاء قوة جاذبة للأجسام، ولذلك يحتبس الماء في السراقات) وينجذب في الزراقات كما مر ( وقال بعضهم : فيه قوة دافعة ) للأجسام ( إلى فوق فإِنَّ التخلخل الواقع في الجسم بسبب كثرة الخلاء في داخله أعني أن يتفرق أجزاؤه ويداخلها خلاء ( يفيد ) ذلك الجسم ( خفة دافعة له إلى الفوق، والجمهور على أنه ليس في الخلاء قوة جاذبة ولا دافعة وهو الحق . المكان قول : ( إلا بعد الجسم الخ) أي السطح الباطن القائم به . قوله : ( وهو الحق) كما بينه الشيخ في الشفاء. قوله : ( فإذا حل في مادة فجسم) أي جسم تعليمي . قوله : ( والجمهور على أنَّه ليس في الخلاء قوة جاذبة ولا دافعة وهو الحق) أما بطلان القول الأول فلان الخلاء لو كان فيه قوة جاذبة للجسم إلى نفسه لكان يجب أن يمسكه عند وصوله إليه وإن لا يمكنه من أن يفارقه وينفصل عنه على أن ابن زكريا إن أراد خلاء موهوماً فلا خلاء في السراقات حال الشغل بالماء، وإن أراد خلاء موجوداً فما الفرق بين السراقات وغيرها، وأما بطلان القول الثاني فلان الخلاء متشابه الأجزاء كما سبق فليس بعض أجزائه بالدفع منه إلى آخر أولى من العكس فيلزم أن لا يسكن الجسم في الخلاء.
(So it is a void) meaning an existing dimension abstract from matter, whether it is occupied by a bodily dimension that fills it or unoccupied, for it is in itself a void. And some of them made it a pure non-existence as mentioned, that it is the reality of the void according to those who say that place is an imagined dimension such that two bodies do not meet and there is nothing between them that meets them. The second (among them), i.e. among the proponents of the void, namely the existing dimension abstract from matter, (is that) some allowed that no body fills it, in which case it is a void in the sense that it is a dimension abstract from matter and in the sense that it is a place empty of an occupier. And some of them did not allow that, in which case it is a void in the first sense without the second. The doctrine of the one who says that it is a surface is that between the edges of the bowl, according to this doctrine, there is an existing dimension abstract from matter upon which the dimension of the body has superimposed; so there are two dimensions, except that the first cannot be devoid of the superimposition of the second upon it. But according to the statement that it is the surface, there is only the dimension of the body that is inside the bowl. The third: Ibn Zakariyya said about the void that it has an attractive force for bodies, and therefore water is retained in pipes and drawn in siphons as mentioned. And some said that it has a repulsive force for bodies upward, for the rarefaction occurring in the body due to the abundance of void inside it, meaning that its parts become separated and a void enters them, gives that body lightness pushing it upward. And the majority holds that the void has no attractive or repulsive force, and this is the truth. Place. His statement: (except the dimension of the body etc.) meaning the internal surface subsisting in it. His statement: (and it is the truth) as the Shaykh explained in al-Shifa'. His statement: (so if it enters matter, it becomes a body) i.e. a mathematical body. His statement: (and the majority holds that the void has no attractive or repulsive force, and this is the truth): As for the falsity of the first statement, because if the void had an attractive force drawing the body to itself, it would have to hold it when it reaches it and not allow it to separate and detach from it. Moreover, if Ibn Zakariyya meant an imagined void, there is no void in the pipes when they are filled with water; if he meant an existing void, what is the difference between pipes and other things? As for the falsity of the second statement, because the void is uniform in its parts as previously mentioned, so no part of it is more deserving of pushing to another than the opposite, and therefore the body would not come to rest in the void.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage defines the void (khala') and refutes two false views about forces in the void. The author begins by defining khala' as an existing dimension abstract from matter, whether occupied by a body's dimension or not. He mentions that some thinkers deny its existence, but the discussion proceeds with the view that it exists. Two variants among proponents are noted: some allow it to be empty of a body, others do not. Then the author presents the surface theory as an alternative. Next, he introduces Ibn Zakariyya's claim that the void has an attractive force, explaining that water remains in pipes due to this attraction. Another claim is that the void has a repulsive force, causing rarefaction that makes bodies lighter and push upward. The majority, however, correctly holds that the void has no such forces. The commentary then elaborates: the falsity of the attractive force view is shown because if the void attracted bodies, it would hold them and not let go; also, if Ibn Zakariyya meant an imagined void, there is no void in filled pipes; if an existing one, why distinguish pipes? The repulsive force view is false because the void is uniform, so no part would push more than another, leading to no body being at rest. Thus the correct view is established: the void is passive with no inherent forces.

خلاء
بعد موجود مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the void is pure nothingness or non-existence.
Correction: The void is an existing dimension abstract from matter, not a pure absence.

Refuting Attractive and Repulsive Forces in the Void
Al-Iji presents and refutes two erroneous views about forces in the void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the majority of scholars reject claims that the void contains attractive or repulsive forces, and the logical arguments against each view.

Core Idea
The void is passive and uniform; it cannot attract or repel bodies.

Al-Iji now addresses two false claims about the void: that it has an attractive force that holds water in pipes, and that it has a repulsive force causing lightness. He refutes both with simple logical arguments, establishing the mainstream position that the void has no inherent forces.

Guided Path
1
Read the two claims
Read Ibn Zakariyya's claim of an attractive force and the other claim of a repulsive force. Note how each is supposedly evidenced.
2
Study the refutation of attractive force
Understand why an attractive force would prevent bodies from leaving the void, and why Ibn Zakariyya's view is inconsistent.
The refutation uses a dilemma about whether the void is imagined or existing.
3
Study the refutation of repulsive force
Understand why uniformity of the void implies no directional push, leading to bodies never resting.
4
Conclude with the majority view
Affirm that the void has no attractive or repulsive force, as the majority holds.
This view is declared 'the truth'.

Source Text

( الثالث قال ابن زكريا في الخلاء قوة جاذبة للأجسام، ولذلك يحتبس الماء في السراقات) وينجذب في الزراقات كما مر ( وقال بعضهم : فيه قوة دافعة ) للأجسام ( إلى فوق فإِنَّ التخلخل الواقع في الجسم بسبب كثرة الخلاء في داخله أعني أن يتفرق أجزاؤه ويداخلها خلاء ( يفيد ) ذلك الجسم ( خفة دافعة له إلى الفوق، والجمهور على أنه ليس في الخلاء قوة جاذبة ولا دافعة وهو الحق . المكان قول : ( إلا بعد الجسم الخ) أي السطح الباطن القائم به . قوله : ( وهو الحق) كما بينه الشيخ في الشفاء. قوله : ( فإذا حل في مادة فجسم) أي جسم تعليمي . قوله : ( والجمهور على أنَّه ليس في الخلاء قوة جاذبة ولا دافعة وهو الحق) أما بطلان القول الأول فلان الخلاء لو كان فيه قوة جاذبة للجسم إلى نفسه لكان يجب أن يمسكه عند وصوله إليه وإن لا يمكنه من أن يفارقه وينفصل عنه على أن ابن زكريا إن أراد خلاء موهوماً فلا خلاء في السراقات حال الشغل بالماء، وإن أراد خلاء موجوداً فما الفرق بين السراقات وغيرها، وأما بطلان القول الثاني فلان الخلاء متشابه الأجزاء كما سبق فليس بعض أجزائه بالدفع منه إلى آخر أولى من العكس فيلزم أن لا يسكن الجسم في الخلاء.
(So it is a void) meaning an existing dimension abstract from matter, whether it is occupied by a bodily dimension that fills it or unoccupied, for it is in itself a void. And some of them made it a pure non-existence as mentioned, that it is the reality of the void according to those who say that place is an imagined dimension such that two bodies do not meet and there is nothing between them that meets them. The second (among them), i.e. among the proponents of the void, namely the existing dimension abstract from matter, (is that) some allowed that no body fills it, in which case it is a void in the sense that it is a dimension abstract from matter and in the sense that it is a place empty of an occupier. And some of them did not allow that, in which case it is a void in the first sense without the second. The doctrine of the one who says that it is a surface is that between the edges of the bowl, according to this doctrine, there is an existing dimension abstract from matter upon which the dimension of the body has superimposed; so there are two dimensions, except that the first cannot be devoid of the superimposition of the second upon it. But according to the statement that it is the surface, there is only the dimension of the body that is inside the bowl. The third: Ibn Zakariyya said about the void that it has an attractive force for bodies, and therefore water is retained in pipes and drawn in siphons as mentioned. And some said that it has a repulsive force for bodies upward, for the rarefaction occurring in the body due to the abundance of void inside it, meaning that its parts become separated and a void enters them, gives that body lightness pushing it upward. And the majority holds that the void has no attractive or repulsive force, and this is the truth. Place. His statement: (except the dimension of the body etc.) meaning the internal surface subsisting in it. His statement: (and it is the truth) as the Shaykh explained in al-Shifa'. His statement: (so if it enters matter, it becomes a body) i.e. a mathematical body. His statement: (and the majority holds that the void has no attractive or repulsive force, and this is the truth): As for the falsity of the first statement, because if the void had an attractive force drawing the body to itself, it would have to hold it when it reaches it and not allow it to separate and detach from it. Moreover, if Ibn Zakariyya meant an imagined void, there is no void in the pipes when they are filled with water; if he meant an existing void, what is the difference between pipes and other things? As for the falsity of the second statement, because the void is uniform in its parts as previously mentioned, so no part of it is more deserving of pushing to another than the opposite, and therefore the body would not come to rest in the void.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage defines the void (khala') and refutes two false views about forces in the void. The author begins by defining khala' as an existing dimension abstract from matter, whether occupied by a body's dimension or not. He mentions that some thinkers deny its existence, but the discussion proceeds with the view that it exists. Two variants among proponents are noted: some allow it to be empty of a body, others do not. Then the author presents the surface theory as an alternative. Next, he introduces Ibn Zakariyya's claim that the void has an attractive force, explaining that water remains in pipes due to this attraction. Another claim is that the void has a repulsive force, causing rarefaction that makes bodies lighter and push upward. The majority, however, correctly holds that the void has no such forces. The commentary then elaborates: the falsity of the attractive force view is shown because if the void attracted bodies, it would hold them and not let go; also, if Ibn Zakariyya meant an imagined void, there is no void in filled pipes; if an existing one, why distinguish pipes? The repulsive force view is false because the void is uniform, so no part would push more than another, leading to no body being at rest. Thus the correct view is established: the void is passive with no inherent forces.

Key Terms
قوة جاذبة
قوة دافعة
تخلخل
متشابه الأجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the void can actively attract or repel bodies.
Correction: The void is passive and uniform; it has no inherent forces.

Checkpoint
Why is the repulsive force view considered false?
A) Because it contradicts scripture
B) Because the void is uniform, so no part pushes more than another
C) Because bodies are always at rest
D) Because rarefaction is impossible

Key Takeaway
The void has no active forces; it is uniform and passive, so attractive and repulsive forces cannot exist in it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

Critique of Defining Heat by Its Effects
A critique of Ibn Sina's definition of heat as circular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining heat by its effects is circular according to the author's critique of Ibn Sina.

Core Idea
Defining heat by its effects is circular because the effects already presuppose knowledge of heat.

In this passage, the author critiques Ibn Sina's attempt to define heat by its effects, arguing that such a definition is circular. He presents two main reasons: the essence of heat is more apparent than its effects, and knowledge of the effects depends on prior knowledge of heat. The passage also addresses an objection and provides an answer based on the role of sensation in knowing essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to grasp the argument. Note the structure: critique, reasons, objection, answer.
2
Identify the two reasons against the definition
The author gives two reasons why the definition is flawed. Find them in the text.
3
Understand the objection and the response
The author anticipates an objection and answers it. Follow the logical flow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جعل هذا الذي ذكره ابن سينا من أحوال الحرارة تعريفاً للحرارة فقد ركب شططاً لأن ماهيتها أوضح من ذلك المذكور، فإن كثيراً من الناس يعرفونها مع عدم شعورهم بما ذكر حكمها ولأن ذلك الحكم المذكور الذي هو الآثار المخصوصة لا يعلم إلا باستقراء جزئياتها فإنها ما لم تستقرأ جزئياتها لم يعرف كون هذه الآثار خاصة شاملة لها فمعرفتها موقوفة على معرفة الحرارة، فتعريفها بهذه الآثار دور. لا يقال: يكفينا في تتبع جزئياتها والاطلاع على أحوالها المذكورة معرفة الحرارة بوجه ما فإذا عرفت بها أفادت معرفتها بوجه أكمل، فلا دور؛ لأنا نقول الإحساس بجزئياتها كاف في معرفة ماهيتها ألا ترى إلى ما ذكره أن المحسوسات لا يجوز تعريفها بالأقوال الشارحة. إذ لا يمكن أن تعرف إلا بإضافات واعتبارات لازمية لها، لا يفيد شيء منها معرفة حقائقها مثل ما تفيده الإحساسات بجزئياتها فالمقصود بذكر خواصها وآثارها في بيان حقائقها مزيد تمييز لها عما عداها لا تصور ماهيتها
And whoever makes what Ibn Sina mentioned of the states of heat a definition of heat has certainly gone far astray, because its essence is clearer than that mentioned. Indeed, many people know it without being aware of the rule he mentioned. And because that mentioned rule, which is the specific effects, is not known except by induction of its particulars. For if its particulars are not surveyed, it is not known that these effects are specific and comprehensive to it. So knowledge of them is dependent on knowledge of heat, so defining it by these effects is circular. It cannot be said: it suffices us in tracing its particulars and learning its mentioned states to know heat in some way, so if it is known by them, it provides knowledge in a more complete way, so no circularity; because we say: sensation of its particulars is sufficient for knowing its essence. Do you not see what he mentioned that sensibles cannot be defined by explanatory statements? For they cannot be known except through additional necessary relations and considerations, none of which provides knowledge of their realities like what the sensations of their particulars provide. So the purpose of mentioning its properties and effects in explaining its realities is further distinction for it from others, not conceptualization of its essence.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a critique of Ibn Sina's attempt to define heat by its effects. The author argues that defining heat by its effects is circular because knowledge of those effects requires prior knowledge of heat. He then presents an objection: one might know heat in a simple way and through its effects gain a more complete knowledge, thus avoiding circularity. The answer is that sensation of particulars is sufficient to know the essence; explanatory definitions cannot convey reality like sensation does. Therefore, mentioning effects serves only to further distinguish heat, not to define its essence.

Key Terms
الحرارة
التعريف
الدور
الإحساس
المحسوسات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Defining a thing by its effects is always a valid definition.
Correction: If the effects are only known through the thing itself, the definition becomes circular. For sensibles like heat, direct sensation provides knowledge of essence, not definitions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is defining heat by its effects circular?
A) Because heat's effects are unknown
B) Because knowing the effects presupposes knowledge of heat
C) Because heat is a simple substance
D) Because the effects are not universal

Key Takeaway
Defining a sensible thing like heat by its effects is circular because the effects already depend on the thing itself.

Conditions for Heat's Effect on Composite Bodies
Conditions for heat's effect on composite bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when heat separates composite bodies and when it melts them, based on the strength of fusion and the quantities of parts.

Core Idea
Heat separates weak composites but melts strong ones with balanced parts.

This passage qualifies the earlier claim about heat's effects. Not all composites react the same: the strength of cohesion determines whether heat separates or melts. The example of gold illustrates the melting scenario.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then' clauses.
Pay attention to the word 'التحام' (cohesion) and 'التفريق' (separation).
2
Identify the two conditions
Find where the author states the condition for separation (weak fusion) and the condition for non-separation (strong fusion). Determine what happens in each case.
3
Analyze the melting example
Consider the example of gold. Why does heat cause melting rather than separation? Relate it to the 'close in quantity' condition.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنَّ هذا الذي ذكرناه من آثار الحرارة في الجسم المركب من الأجزاء المختلفة في اللطافة والكثافة إنما يثبت إذا لم يكن الالتئام بين بسائط ذلك المركب شديداً حتى يمكن تفريق بعضها عن بعض وأما إذا اشتد الالتحام بين تلك البسائط وقوي التركيب فيما بينها فالنار بحرارتها لا تفرقها لوجود المانع عن التفريق. وحينئذ فإن كانت الأجزاء اللطيفة والكثيفة ذلك الجسم متقاربة في الكمية كما في الذهب أفادته الحرارة سيلاناً وذوباناً
And know that this which we have mentioned of the effects of heat on the composite body consisting of parts differing in subtlety and density is only established if the fusion between the simples of that composite is not strong, so that it is possible to separate some from others. But if the cohesion between those simples becomes intense and the composition among them becomes strong, then fire, by its heat, does not separate them due to the presence of a hindrance to separation. And then, if the subtle and dense parts of that body are close in quantity, as in gold, heat causes it fluidity and melting.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage qualifies the earlier claim about heat's effect of separating different parts. The author states that this separation occurs only when the fusion between components is not strong. If the cohesion is strong, heat cannot separate them. In such cases, if the subtle and dense parts are similar in quantity (e.g., gold), heat causes melting instead of separation.

Key Terms
الالتئام
التفريق
السيلان
الذهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Heat always separates the components of a composite body.
Correction: Heat only separates when fusion is weak; when fusion is strong, it may cause melting if the parts are balanced in quantity.

Checkpoint

A) When the parts are very subtle
B) When the fusion is strong
C) When the body is liquid
D) When the heat is weak

Key Takeaway
The effect of heat on composite bodies depends on the strength of fusion and the relative quantities of the components.

Effect of Heat on Composite Bodies: The Obstacle
The author explains when heat separates components and when it only causes melting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition under which heat separates composite bodies, the phenomenon of rotational motion in gold, and why the non-occurrence of separation does not disprove heat's separating power.

Core Idea
Effect blocked by obstacle does not negate power.

This passage from a classic work on natural philosophy explains how heat interacts with composite bodies. The author first sets a condition: separation only happens when the body's parts are not too tightly bound. He then uses gold as an example to show what occurs when parts are tightly bound—melting and a curious rotational motion. Finally, he addresses a common misunderstanding: just because heat does not separate gold, that does not mean it lacks the power to do so, for an obstacle can prevent the effect. This lesson will guide you through the text and its logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text and its English translation carefully. Pay attention to the structure: condition, example, defense.
2
Identify the condition for separation
Find the sentence that states when heat can separate parts. Note the term ‘الالتئام’ (cohesion).
3
Analyze the gold example
Understand why gold does not separate but rather rotates. Notice the explanation about fine and coarse parts and mutual resistance.
4
Grasp the logical point about obstacles
The author argues that absence of effect does not indicate absence of cause. Reflect on why this is logically necessary.
5
Apply the concept
Think of another situation where an effect is prevented by an obstacle, and consider how this principle applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذا الذي ذكرناه من آثار الحرارة في الجسم المركب من الأجزاء المختلفة في اللطافة والكثافة إنما يثبت إذا لم يكن الالتئام بين بسائط ذلك المركب شديداً حتى يمكن تفريق بعضها عن بعض. وأما إذا اشتد الالتحام بين تلك البسائط وقوي التركيب فيما بينها فالنار بحرارتها لا تفرقها لوجود المانع عن التفريق. وحينئذ فإن كانت الأجزاء اللطيفة والكثيفة ذلك الجسم متقاربة في الكمية كما في الذهب أفادته الحرارة سيلاناً وذوباناً وكلما حاول اللطيف الخفيف صعوداً منعه الكثيف الثقيل عن ذلك فحدث بينهما تمانع وتجاذب فيحدث من ذلك حركة دوران كما نشاهد في الذهب من حركته السريعة العجيبة في البوتقة ولولا هذا العائق أعني شدة الالتئام والالتحام بين أجزاء الذهب لفرقها النار كما تفرق أجزاء جسم لا يشتد التحامها. وليس عدم الفعل الذي هو التفريق لوجود العائق عن ذلك الفعل في الذهب ونظائره دليلاً على أن النار ليس فيها قوة التفريق بحرارتها، لأن تخلف الفعل عن المقتضى بسبب ما يمنعه منه جائز بالضرورة.
Know that what we have mentioned of the effects of heat in a body composed of parts differing in fineness and coarseness is only established if the cohesion between the simples of that composite is not severe, so that some can be separated from others. But if the fusion between those simples is intense and the composition is strong among them, then fire by its heat does not separate them due to the presence of an obstacle preventing separation. Then, if the fine and coarse parts of that body are close in quantity, as in gold, heat gives it fluidity and melting; and whenever the fine, light part attempts to rise, the coarse, heavy part prevents it, so there occurs between them mutual resistance and attraction, resulting in a rotational motion, as we observe in gold its swift, wondrous motion in the crucible. Were it not for this obstacle—i.e., the intensity of fusion and cohesion between gold's parts—fire would separate it as it separates the parts of a body that does not have intense cohesion. And the absence of the act, which is separation, due to the existence of an obstacle to that act in gold and its likes is not evidence that fire does not possess the power of separation by its heat, because the effect's failing to follow from the requirer due to something that prevents it is [logically] possible by necessity.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a classification and causal explanation of how heat affects composite bodies. The author first states the condition: heat separates parts only when the composite's simples are not too tightly bound. If the binding is strong, heat does not separate them but causes a rotational motion (as in gold) due to mutual resistance between fine and coarse parts. The author then addresses a potential misinterpretation: the fact that heat does not separate gold does not prove it lacks the power to do so, because an effect can be prevented by an obstacle—a point of necessary possibility. The order is: condition, example (gold), demonstration of rotation, and logical defense against inferring absence of power from absence of effect.

Key Terms
بسائط
التركيب
الالتئام
لطافة
كثافة
عائق
دوران
سيلان
ذوبان
مقتضى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an agent does not produce its effect, it must lack the power.
Correction: The effect can be prevented by an obstacle, so absence of effect does not prove absence of power.

Checkpoint
What happens when a body like gold is heated and its parts are tightly bound?
A) The heat separates the parts immediately.
B) The body becomes liquid and rotates due to mutual resistance.
C) The body remains unchanged.
D) The fine parts ascend and leave the coarse behind.

Key Takeaway
The absence of an effect does not prove the absence of power; an obstacle can prevent the effect.

Heat Effects and Component Dominance
Classification of heat's effects based on dominance of fine or coarse parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the relative dominance of fine and coarse components in a body determines the effect of heat, and be able to identify the three cases with examples.

Core Idea
Heat's effect depends on which part—fine or coarse—dominates in the composite.

The author now applies the principle of predominance to explain how heat affects composite bodies. Rather than a uniform response, heat produces different outcomes based on whether the fine or coarse parts dominate. This passage classifies those outcomes using intuitive examples like sal ammoniac and iron.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the three conditions and examples.
The text ends abruptly after the third condition; infer the likely outcome.
2
Identify the three cases
Find each case: (1) fine greatly dominates, (2) coarse dominates but not greatly, (3) coarse greatly dominates. Note the effect for each.
The third case is incomplete; think about what might happen.
3
Understand the examples
Sal ammoniac exemplifies the first case; iron exemplifies the second. Consider why these examples were chosen.
4
Note the variant reading
Some manuscripts have 'due to its dominance' instead of 'due to its scarcity'. Reflect on how this changes the emphasis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن غلب اللطيف على الكثيف جداً أي غلبة تامة فيصعد اللطيف حينئذ ويستصحب معه الكثيف لقلته أي قلة الكثيف وفي بعض النسخ لغلبته أي لغلبة اللطيف على الكثيف كالنوشادر فإنه إذا أثرت فيه الحرارة صعد بالكلية أو لا يغلب اللطيف بل يغلب الكثيف لكن لا يكون غالباً جداً فتفيده الحرارة إذا أثرت فيه تلييناً كما في الحديد. وإن غلب الكثيف جداً
And if the fine [part] greatly dominates the coarse—i.e., complete dominance—then the fine ascends at that time and carries the coarse with it due to its scarcity, i.e., the scarcity of the coarse, and in some copies 'due to its dominance' i.e., due to the fine's dominance over the coarse, like sal ammoniac, for when heat affects it, it ascends entirely. Or the fine does not dominate, but the coarse dominates, but not very much, so heat, when it affects it, yields softening, as in iron. And if the coarse dominates greatly...
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a classification of heat's effects based on the relative dominance of fine and coarse parts in a composite body. The author presents three cases: (1) If the fine part dominates greatly, it ascends entirely, carrying the coarse with it, as with sal ammoniac. (2) If the coarse dominates but not greatly, heat only softens the body, as with iron. (3) If the coarse dominates greatly—the text cuts off, but the implication is that no significant effect occurs. The classification is enumerative, listing each case with an example. The author also notes a variant reading in some copies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think heat always separates or melts composite bodies uniformly.
Correction: The author shows that the outcome depends on the balance of fine and coarse parts.

Checkpoint
What happens when heat is applied to sal ammoniac (النوشادر) according to the author?
A) It melts and rotates.
B) It softens.
C) It ascends entirely with the fine parts carrying the coarse.
D) It remains unchanged.

Key Takeaway
The effect of heat on a composite body is determined by whether fine or coarse parts dominate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Objection and Answer: Heat and the Separation of Similars
Raises an objection about heat separating similar water parts and joining different egg parts, and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a specific objection against the rule that heat separates similars and joins dissimilars is answered through the concept of transformation and incidental effects.

Core Idea
Apparent counterexamples to the rule are explained by transformation, not direct action.

This lesson presents an objection to a general claim about the behavior of fire. The claim is that fire separates different things and aggregates similar ones. The objection gives two examples – water and egg – that seem to contradict this. The answer provides a deeper analysis of heat's action, distinguishing direct effects from incidental ones. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating causal claims in natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial claim
Recognize the general rule that fire separates dissimilar things and aggregates similar things. This rule is being challenged.
The rule was established in a previous lesson.
2
Examine the objection
Identify the two counterexamples: water parts are similar yet heat separates them by evaporation; egg yolk and white are different yet heat makes them cohere more. These seem to disprove the rule.
Note that the objection claims both judgments are invalid.
3
Study the answer
Follow the reasoning that heat's action on water is transformation into air, not direct separation. The separation is incidental. For the egg, the components are not similar, so the rule about joining similars does not apply.
The answer preserves the original rule by reinterpreting the examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يورد (عليه أي على ما ذكرنا من أن النار تفرق المختلفات، وتجمع المتماثلات كأجزاء الماء ) فإنها متماثلة ( وتصعدها ) الحرارة ( بالتبخير) فتفرق بعضها عن بعض، وقد تجمع) الحرارة المختلفات كصفرة البيض وبياضه، فإنَّ الحرارة إذا أثرت فيهما زادتهما تلازماً واجتماعاً مع تخالفهما فلا يصح شيء من ذينك الحكمين (ويجاب بأن فعلها في الماء إحالة إلى الهواء فإنَّ الحرارة إذا أثرت في الماء انقلب بعضه هواء، وتحرك بطبعه إلى الفوق . ثم إنه يختلط ويلتزق بذلك الهواء أجزاء مائية فتصعد معه، ويكون ذلك بخاراً ففعل الحرارة في الماء إحالة إلى الهواء، (لا تفريق) بين أجزائه المتماثلة. قوله: (فلا يصح إلخ ) قال الشارح قُدس سره في حواشي شرح طوالع الأصفهاني: هذا الحسكمان إذا أثرت الحرارة في الجسم المركب من الأجسام المختلفة لطافة وكثافة وربما أثرت الجسم البسيط كالماء فأفادت تفريق المتمائلات وجمع في قوله: ( ثم إنه يختلط إلخ) أشار بإيراد كلمة ثم إلى أنَّ الاختلاط والالتزاق ليس ناشئاً من الإحالة والتفريق بين الهواء والماء بل هو أمر اتفاقي.
And sometimes it is objected (against what we mentioned that fire separates different things and aggregates similar things, like parts of water) for they are similar, and heat rarefies them by evaporation, so it separates some from others. And heat may aggregate different things, like the yolk and white of an egg, for when heat affects them it increases their cohesion and union despite their heterogeneity. So neither of these two judgments is universally valid. (And it is answered that its action in water is transformation into air, for when heat affects water, some of it turns into air and moves upward by its nature. Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the earlier claim that fire separates different things and aggregates similar things. The objection gives two counterexamples: first, water parts are similar, yet heat separates them by evaporation; second, the yolk and white of an egg are different, yet heat makes them cohere more. Thus the general rule seems false. The answer explains: heat's action on water is not direct separation but transformation into air; some water turns into air and rises, and some water particles adhere to that air, so the separation is not the primary action of heat but a secondary effect of the transformation. The commentator adds that the mixing and adhesion of water particles to air is accidental, not caused by the transformation. Thus the objection is refuted: the separation of water's similar parts is not heat's direct action, and the aggregation of egg yolk and white is not a general counterexample because it involves different components where heat increases cohesion, but that is not the same as aggregating similars. The discussion shows the nuanced way in which the earlier claim is defended.

Objection and Answer on Heat's Effect
Objection and answer regarding heat's effects on similar and different substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the principle that fire separates different things and brings together similar things against an objection based on water and egg, and how causality is analyzed in terms of direct vs indirect effects.

Core Idea
Heat's primary action is transformation, not direct separation or collection.

This passage presents a classic objection to the principle that fire separates different substances and collects similar ones. The objector points to water and egg as counterexamples. The answer requires careful analysis of causation: heat's primary action is transformation, and apparent separation or collection are indirect effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the claim that fire separates different things and brings together similar things. Then note the counterexamples: water (similar parts separated by evaporation) and egg (different parts brought together by cooking).
2
Understand the answer for water
The answer states that heat transforms some water into air, and water particles adhere to that air, rising together. The separation is not direct but a result of transformation and adhesion.
Focus on the phrase 'إحالة إلى الهواء' (transformation into air).
3
Understand the answer for egg
The answer explains that heat thickens the consistency of yolk and white, but the union between them existed before heating. Heat does not bring them together; it only alters consistency.
The commentator adds that heat will eventually separate them by distillation.
4
Reflect on direct vs indirect causation
Consider how this argument preserves the principle by distinguishing between what heat does directly (transformation) and what happens as a secondary effect.
Think about how the commentator addresses the objection that separation of similars also occurs via heat.

The commentator said: (By distillation) i.e., by distilling the watery parts from it.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer concerning the effects of heat on similar and different substances. The objector argues that if fire supposedly separates different things and brings together similar ones, counterexamples exist: heat separates similar parts of water by evaporation, and brings together dissimilar parts like egg yolk and white. The answer distinguishes between direct and indirect effects. In water, heat transforms some parts into air, which carries water particles upward; the separation of water particles is not caused directly by heat but by adhesion to the rising air. In egg, heat thickens the consistency; the union of yolk and white was already present before heating. Thus heat's primary action is transformation, not separation or collection. The commentator further clarifies that the separation of similar parts occurs through heat's mediation, not as its direct act, resolving the objection.

Motion and Heat: Proof, Objection, and Answer
Al-Jurjani presents the proof, objection, and answer regarding motion generating heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that motion generates heat, Abu al-Barakat's objection based on celestial spheres, and the two-part answer regarding the need for a receptive subject and the smoothness of surfaces.

Core Idea
Motion needs a receptive subject to produce heat; celestial matter lacks it.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani presents the fourth argument that motion generates heat, confirmed by experience. He then reports a powerful objection from Abu al-Barakat: if motion causes heat, the swift celestial spheres should be scorching hot, and the elements near them should ignite. The answer reveals a key principle of causal reasoning: an effect requires both an efficient cause and a receptive subject. We will trace this nuanced exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has three distinct parts: proof, objection, answer.
2
Understand the empirical claim
The passage opens with the claim that motion generates heat, and that experience confirms it. This is the starting point of the argument.
Think about everyday examples like friction.
3
Analyze Abu al-Barakat's objection
Abu al-Barakat argues that if motion causes heat, then the celestial spheres (which move rapidly) should be extremely hot, and the nearby elements should ignite. This seems to contradict observation.
Consider why this objection is powerful: it uses the same principle to predict an absurd outcome.
4
Examine the two-part answer
The answer first states that the spheres' matter does not accept heat at all—heat requires a receptive subject. Second, the elements do not move with the spheres due to smooth surfaces, so they are not heated by motion. Then a refined answer: only fire follows the sphere, but its heat is resisted by the cold layer.
Note the logical structure: the answer does not deny the causal link between motion and heat, but adds the condition of a receptive subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعها أن الحركة تحدث الحرارة والتجربة تحققه. وقد أنكره أبو البركات وإليه الإشارة بقوله: إذا كانت الحركة تحدث الحرارة فيجب أن تسخن الأفلاك سخونة شديدة جداً بواسطة حركاتها السريعة، ويتسخن بمجاورتها العناصر الثلاثة التي هي في وسط الأثير، والأفلاك بمنزلة القطرة في البحر المحيط، فيصير هذه الثلاثة كلها بالتدريج ناراً لاستيلاء سخونة الأفلاك عليها مع مساعدة كرة الأثير إياها في تسخينها. والجواب أن مواد الأفلاك لا تقبل السخونة أصلاً، فلا بد في وجود الحرارة من المقتضي الذي هو الحركة ومن وجود القابل. وحينئذ فلا تسخن الأفلاك بسبب حركتها، فلا تسخن العناصر بالمجاورة. وليست العناصر متحركة على سبيل التبعية فإنها لملاسة سطوحها لا تتحرك بحركة الأفلاك فتسخن. ولهم كلام مناقض لهذا أن العناصر لا تتحرك بحركة الأفلاك، فسيأتيك في موقف الجواهر أنهم قالوا: النار تتحرك بتبعية الفلك، وليس التحريك يتعين أن يكون بالتشبث فيمنعها ملاسة السطوح، فإن الأفلاك عندهم يحرك بعضها بعضاً، ولا خشونة في سطوحها لتكون متشبثة بسببها. فالأولى في الجواب أن يقال: النار متحركة بمتابعة الفلك دون باقي العناصر، وليس سخونة النار توجب سخونة الباقي لأن برودة الطبقة الزمهريرية تقاومها.
The fourth [point] is that motion generates heat, and experience confirms it. But Abu al-Barakat denied it, and to him is the indication by his saying: If motion generates heat, then the spheres must become extremely hot by virtue of their rapid motions, and the three elements that are in the middle of the ether must become hot by their adjacency, and the spheres are like a drop in the encompassing sea, so all of these three would gradually become fire due to the heat of the spheres overcoming them, with the help of the sphere of ether in heating them. The answer: that the materials of the spheres do not accept heat at all, and for the existence of heat there must be the efficient cause, which is motion, and the presence of a recipient. Thus the spheres do not become hot due to their motion, so the elements do not become hot by adjacency. And the elements do not move in the way of following, because, due to the smoothness of their surfaces, they do not move with the motion of the spheres and so become hot. And they have a contradictory statement that the elements do not move with the motion of the spheres; it will come to you in the topic of substances that they said: Fire moves in following the sphere, and movement is not necessarily by clinging such that the smoothness of the surfaces would prevent it, for the spheres according to them move one another, and there is no roughness on their surfaces by which they would cling. So the better in the answer is to say: Fire moves in pursuit of the sphere without the rest of the elements, and the heat of fire does not necessitate the heat of the rest because the cold of the frozen layer resists it.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument concerning heat: motion produces heat, and this is empirically confirmed. Al-Jurjani then reports the objection of Abu al-Barakat: if motion causes heat, then the celestial spheres should be extremely hot due to their swift motions, and the three elements (probably fire, air, and water) would be heated by proximity, eventually turning them all into fire. The answer has two parts. First, the matter of the spheres does not have the capacity to receive heat, so even though motion is a necessary cause, the effect also requires a receptive subject, which the spheres lack. Hence they do not become hot. Second, the elements do not move as a consequence of the spheres' motion because their surfaces are smooth, preventing them from being dragged along. Then Al-Jurjani notes a potential contradiction: elsewhere it is stated that fire moves in pursuit of the sphere. The preferred resolution is that only fire follows the sphere, not the other elements, and the heat of fire does not infect the other elements because the cold of the frozen layer (al-zamharir) counteracts it.

Correction: The passage shows that heat generation also requires a receptive subject; the spheres' matter does not accept heat, so motion alone is insufficient.

Key Takeaway
Heat generation requires both motion and a receptive subject; celestial matter lacks the latter, so the spheres remain cool despite rapid motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that motion generates heat only when there is a receptive subject. In your own spiritual life, are there truths you have heard many times (like the importance of humility or gratitude) that have not 'heated' your heart? What might be missing—are you lacking the receptive quality that would allow those truths to affect you? Will you ask Allah to make your heart a receptive subject?
?
Abu al-Barakat assumed that because motion generates heat in common experience, it must do so in the celestial realm. Are there areas of your faith where you are overgeneralizing from your limited experience—assuming something must be true of the unseen world because it is true in the seen world? For example, do you think Allah's mercy or justice operates exactly like human mercy or justice? How can you guard against such overgeneralization?
?
The answer in this passage shows that the same cause (motion) can fail to produce its usual effect if the subject is not receptive. Think of a habit or sin you have struggled with. You may have tried many 'causes' to change—reading, advice, willpower—but if your heart was not truly receptive, change did not come. What would it mean for you to become a receptive subject for Allah's guidance? What barriers (like pride or laziness) must you remove?

Smooth Surfaces and Celestial Heat: An Objection and a Better Answer
Objection and answer regarding heat from sphere motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the smooth surface objection against celestial spheres causing heat and the better answer involving fire's motion and the frigid zone.

Core Idea
Smoothness breaks direct motion transfer, but fire still moves with the sphere, and cold opposes heat.

This passage from al-Mawaqif presents a classic puzzle: if spheres are smooth, how can they move the elements? And if they do, why don't they heat them? The text offers a refined answer that addresses these questions. Let's explore the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the claim that smooth surfaces prevent motion transfer.
The passage includes an apparent contradiction that is later resolved.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states that since the lunar sphere and fire's convex are smooth, sphere motion does not force element motion, so no heat.
This is based on lack of friction.
3
Identify the contradiction
The text notes that earlier they said fire moves with the sphere, but here they deny element motion. How can both be true?
The resolution is that only fire moves, and not through clinging.
4
Grasp the better answer
The better answer: fire moves in tandem with the sphere, but other elements do not. Furthermore, fire's heat is insufficient to warm the rest because the frigid zone's cold resists.
This preserves the observation that fire moves upward while explaining lack of heat.
5
Reflect on the implications
This shows how medieval thinkers refined causal explanations by distinguishing different types of motion and resistance.
It also demonstrates the use of smoothness to deny friction-based causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الأول : في الحرارة أنه جواب النفي، والحاصل أنَّ مقعر فلك القمر ومحدب النار سطحان أملسان، فلا يلزم من حركة أحدهما حركة الآخر فإذن أجرام الأفلاك ليست متسخنة بحركاتها ولا محركة للعناصر حتى يلزم سخونتها بوجه ما ( ولهم كلام مناقض لهذا الذي ذكروه ها هنا أن العناصر لا تتحرك بحركة الأفلاك ( فسيأتيك ( في موقف الجواهر ( أنهم قالوا : النار تتحرك بتبعية الفلك وليس التحريك يتعين أن يكون بالتشبث فيمنعها ملاسة السطوح ) فإنَّ الأفلاك عندهم يحرك بعضها بعضاً، ولا خشونة في سطوحها لتكون متشبثة بسببها فالأولى في الجواب أن يقال : النار متحركة بمتابعة الفلك دون باقي العناصر وليس سخونة النار توجب سخونة الباقي لأن برودة الطبقة الزمهريرية تقاومها .

قوله : ( على أنه جواب النفي أي لا حركة فلا تسخن. قوله: (وليس التحريك إلخ) هذا الكلام منع للسند فإن المجيب كان مانعاً للزوم حركة العناصر مستنداً بأنها ملساء فيجوز أن لا تتحرك بحركة الأفلاك. قوله : (فالأولى) قد عرفت وجه اختيار لفظ الأولى . قوله: (في الجواب أي عن شبهة لزوم حرارة العناصر بالحركة التبعية لا عن شبهة أبي البركات . قوله : (لأن البرد إلخ) متعلق بالنفي وعلة له . قوله : (النار تتحرك بتبعية الفلك قيل : الحق في هذه المسألة أنها تتحرك لكن لا بتبعية الفلك إذ حركته من نحو الشمال إلى نحو الجنوب، ولو كانت بالتبعية لكانت على موازاة العدل صرح به صاحب نهاية الإدراك فيه . قوله : ( فالأولى في الجواب أن يقال قوله في الجواب متعلق بحسب المعنى بأن يقال : أي الأولى أن يقال في أثناء الجواب : يعني بدل قوله : والعناصر لملاسة سطوحها إلخ وليس هذا جواباً عن تمام سؤال أبي البركات بل عن لزوم السخونة بحسب حركات العناصر.
The third observatory - first objective: On heat. It is an answer to the negation; the summary is that the concave of the lunar sphere and the convex of fire are two smooth surfaces, so motion of one does not necessitate motion of the other. Therefore, the bodies of the spheres are not heated by their motions nor do they move the elements so that their heat would be necessitated in any way. (And they have a contradictory statement here that the elements do not move with the motion of the spheres; it will come to you in the Station of Substances that they said: Fire moves by following the sphere, and moving is not necessarily by clinging; smoothness of surfaces prevents it.) For the spheres, according to them, move each other, and there is no roughness on their surfaces to cling by. So the better answer is to say: Fire moves in tandem with the sphere, not the rest of the elements, and the heat of fire does not necessitate the heat of the rest, because the cold of the frigid zone (al-ṭabaqa al-zamharīriyya) resists it.

His statement: 'on that it is an answer to the negation' meaning no motion so no heating. His statement: 'and moving is not [etc.]' this speech is a denial of the proof; for the respondent was denying the necessary consequence of the motion of the elements, relying on their smoothness, so it is permissible they do not move with the motion of the spheres. His statement: 'the better' you have known the reason for choosing the word 'better'. His statement: 'in the answer' meaning concerning the doubt of the necessity of heat of the elements by the motion of following, not concerning the doubt of Abū al-Barakāt. His statement: 'because the cold [etc.]' is related to the negation and a cause for it. His statement: 'fire moves by following the sphere' it is said: the truth in this matter is that it moves but not by following the sphere, for its motion is from north to south, and if it were by following it would be parallel to the equator, as stated by the author of Nihāyat al-Idrāk. His statement: 'the better in the answer is to say' his statement 'in the answer' is related in meaning: it is better to say in the course of the answer: meaning instead of his statement 'and the elements due to smoothness of their surfaces etc.' and this is not an answer to the entirety of Abū al-Barakāt's question, but about the necessity of heat according to the motions of the elements.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about whether celestial spheres cause heat in the elements through motion. The original text begins by stating that the concave surface of the lunar sphere and the convex surface of fire are both smooth, so motion of one does not force motion of the other. Hence spheres are not heated by their own motion nor do they move the elements to cause heat. However, the text notes a contradiction: earlier they said elements do not move with spheres, but later in the Station of Substances they said fire does move with the sphere. The explanation given is that sphere-to-sphere motion does not require roughness (they move each other despite smoothness). The better answer, the commentator says, is to assert that fire alone moves with the sphere, not the other elements, and fire's heat is insufficient to warm the rest due to the cold of the frigid zone. The commentator then explains each phrase: the initial 'answer to the negation' means no motion thus no heat; the denial of movement of elements is based on smoothness; and the 'better answer' specifically addresses the issue of heat from following motion, not the original doubt of Abū al-Barakāt. The commentator also notes that some authorities hold fire does move but not by following the sphere's motion because its direction differs.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if fire moves with the sphere, then all elements must move, or that smooth surfaces prevent any motion transfer whatsoever.
Correction: The passage shows that fire moves by following, not by being pushed, and that sphere-to-sphere motion does not require roughness. Smoothness only prevents forced motion, not following motion.

Checkpoint
According to the better answer, why does fire's motion not cause other elements to heat up?
A) Because fire is not hot enough.
B) Because the other elements do not move with the sphere.
C) Because the cold of the frigid zone resists the heat.
D) Because the spheres are too far away.

Key Takeaway
The smooth surface objection challenges heat causation, but the better answer restricts fire's motion to following and uses the frigid zone to explain why other elements remain cold.
